title="Oitirmhor.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="background:#efefef;">\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Scots_Gaelic" class="mw-redirect" title="Scots Gaelic">Scots Gaelic</a>:</td>\n<td><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Sloc na B\xc3\xa9iste</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Pronunciation:</td>\n<td><small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Help:IPA/Scottish Gaelic">[\xcb\x88s\xcc\xaal\xcb\xa0\xcc\xaa\xc9\x94\xca\xb0k n\xc9\x99 \xcb\x88pe\xcb\x90\xca\x83t\xca\xb2\xc9\x99]</a></span><small class="nowrap">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Slocnabeiste.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ec/Slocnabeiste.ogg" class="internal" title="Slocnabeiste.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The earliest written references that have survived relating to the islands were made by <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> in his <i>Natural History</i>, where he states that there are 30 <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Hebudes</i></i>, and makes a separate reference to <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dumna&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dumna (page does not exist)">Dumna</a></i></i>, which Watson (1926) concludes is unequivocally the Outer Hebrides. Writing about 80 years later, in 140\xe2\x80\x93150 AD, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>, drawing on the earlier naval expeditions of <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Julius_Agricola" title="Gnaeus Julius Agricola">Agricola</a></i>, also distinguished between the <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Ebudes</i></i>, of which he writes there were only five (and thus possibly meaning the <a href="/wiki/Inner_Hebrides" title="Inner Hebrides">Inner Hebrides</a>) and <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Dumna</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Breeze_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Breeze-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Dumna</i></i> is cognate with the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Celtic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Celtic">Early Celtic</a> <i>dumnos</i> and means the "deep-sea isle".<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Pliny probably took his information from <a href="/wiki/Pytheas" title="Pytheas">Pytheas</a> of <a href="/wiki/Massilia" class="mw-redirect" title="Massilia">Massilia</a> who visited Britain sometime between 322 and 285 BC. It is possible that Ptolemy did as well, as Agricola\'s information about the west coast of Scotland was of poor quality.<sup id="cite_ref-Breeze_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Breeze-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Breeze also suggests that <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Dumna</i> might be <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Harris" title="Lewis and Harris">Lewis and Harris</a>, the largest island of the Outer Hebrides although he conflates this single island with the name "Long Island".<sup id="cite_ref-Breeze_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Breeze-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Watson (1926)  states that the meaning of Ptolemy\'s <i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">Eboudai</i></i> is unknown and that the root may be pre-Celtic.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Murray (1966) claims that Ptolemy\'s <i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">Ebudae</i></i> was originally derived from the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">Havbredey</i></i>, meaning "isles on the edge of the sea". This idea is often repeated but no firm evidence of this derivation has emerged.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other early written references include the flight of the <a href="/wiki/Nemed" title="Nemed">Nemed</a> people from Ireland to <i>Domon</i>, which is mentioned in the 12th-century <i><i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text"><a href="/wiki/Lebor_Gab%C3%A1la_%C3%89renn" title="Lebor Gab\xc3\xa1la \xc3\x89renn">Lebor Gab\xc3\xa1la \xc3\x89renn</a></i></i> and a 13th-century poem concerning <i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text"><a href="/wiki/Raghnall_mac_Gofraidh" class="mw-redirect" title="Raghnall mac Gofraidh">Raghnall mac Gofraidh</a></i>, then the heir to the throne of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Mann and the Isles">Mann and the Isles</a>, who is said to have "broken the gate of <i><i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text">Magh Domhna</i></i>". <i><i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text">Magh Domhna</i></i> means "the plain of Domhna (or Domon)", but the precise meaning of the text is not clear.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_74-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Irish_mythology" title="Irish mythology">Irish mythology</a> the islands were the home of the <a href="/wiki/Fomorians" title="Fomorians">Fomorians</a>, described as "huge and ugly" and "ship men of the sea". They were pirates, extracting tribute from the coasts of Ireland and one of their kings was <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Indech mac D\xc3\xa9 Domnand</i> (i.e. Indech, son of the goddess Domnu, who ruled over the deep seas).<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In modern Gaelic the islands are sometimes referred to collectively as <i><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">An t-Eilean Fada</i></i> (the Long Island)<sup id="cite_ref-Murray_1966_p._2_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_1966_p._2-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> or <i><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Na h-Eileanan a-Muigh</i></i> (the Outer Isles).<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>  <i><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Innse Gall</i></i> (islands of the foreigners or strangers) is also heard occasionally, a name that was originally used by mainland <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Highlanders</a> when the islands were ruled by the Norse.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The individual island and place names in the Outer Hebrides have mixed Gaelic and Norse origins. Various Gaelic terms are used repeatedly:<sup id="cite_ref-MacanT_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacanT-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Gaelic root\n</th>\n<th>Derived forms\n</th>\n<th>Anglicised as\n</th>\n<th>Origin and meaning\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">-aigh</i>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>-ay/-ey\n</td>\n<td>generally from the Norse <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">\xc3\xb8y</i></i> meaning "island"\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">beag</i>\n</td>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">bheag</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">bige</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">bhige</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">beaga</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">bheaga</i>\n</td>\n<td>beg\n</td>\n<td>small\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">dearg</i>\n</td>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">dhearg</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">deirge</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">dheirge</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">deirg</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">dheirg</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">dearga</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">dhearga</i>\n</td>\n<td>derg\n</td>\n<td>red\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">dubh</i>\n</td>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">dhubh</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">duibh</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">dhuibh</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">duibhe</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">dhuibhe</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">dubha</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">dhubha</i>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>black; hidden\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">glas</i>\n</td>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ghlas</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">glais</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ghlais</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">glaise</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ghlaise</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">glasa</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ghlasa</i>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>grey, green\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ear</i>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>east, eastern\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">eilean</i>\n</td>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">eilein</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">eileanan</i>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>from the Norse <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">eyland</i></i> meaning "island"\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">iar</i>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>west, western\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">m\xc3\xb2r</i>\n</td>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">mh\xc3\xb2r</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">m\xc3\xb2ire</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">mh\xc3\xb2ire</i>}, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">m\xc3\xb2ra</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">mh\xc3\xb2ra</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">m\xc3\xb2ir</i>, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">mh\xc3\xb2ir</i>\n</td>\n<td>more\n</td>\n<td>big, great\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">rubha</i>\n</td>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">rubhannan</i>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>promontory\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">sgeir</i>\n</td>\n<td><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">sgeirean</i>\n</td>\n<td>skerry\n</td>\n<td>skerry; often refers to a rock or rocks that lie submerged at high tide.\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>There are also <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_called_Oronsay" title="List of islands called Oronsay">several islands called <i><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Orasaigh</i></i></a> from the Norse <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">\xc3\x96rfirirsey</i></i> meaning "tidal" or "ebb island".<sup id="cite_ref-MacanT_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacanT-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In Scotland, the Celtic <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> way of life, often troubled but never extinguished by Rome, re-asserted itself when the legions abandoned any permanent occupation in 211 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Hanson (2003) writes: "For many years it has been almost axiomatic in studies of the period that the Roman conquest must have had some major medium or long-term impact on Scotland. On present evidence that cannot be substantiated either in terms of environment, economy, or, indeed, society. The impact appears to have been very limited. The general picture remains one of broad continuity, not of disruption&#160;... The Roman presence in Scotland was little more than a series of brief interludes within a longer continuum of indigenous development."<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The Romans\' direct impact on the Highlands and Islands was scant and there is no evidence that they ever actually landed in the Outer Hebrides.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The later <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> inhabitants of the northern and western Hebrides were probably <a href="/wiki/Pict" class="mw-redirect" title="Pict">Pictish</a>, although the historical record is sparse. Hunter (2000) states that in relation to King <a href="/wiki/Bridei_I_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="Bridei I of the Picts">Bridei I of the Picts</a> in the sixth century AD: "As for Shetland, Orkney, Skye and the Western Isles, their inhabitants, most of whom appear to have been Pictish in culture and speech at this time, are likely to have regarded Bridei as a fairly distant presence."<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The island of Pabbay is the site of the Pabbay Stone, the only extant <a href="/wiki/Pictish_stones" class="mw-redirect" title="Pictish stones">Pictish symbol stone</a> in the Outer Hebrides. This 6th century <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a> shows a flower, V-rod and lunar crescent to which has been added a later and somewhat crude cross.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Norse_control">Norse control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Norse control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Outer_Hebrides" title="History of the Outer Hebrides">History of the Outer Hebrides</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg/220px-Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg.png" width="220" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg/330px-Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg/440px-Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1297" data-file-height="1362" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Location of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Isles" title="Kingdom of the Isles">Kingdom of the Isles</a> at the end of the eleventh century</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> raids began on Scottish shores towards the end of the 8th century AD and the Hebrides came under Norse control and settlement during the ensuing decades, especially following the success of <a href="/wiki/Harald_I_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Harald I of Norway">Harald Fairhair</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hafrsfjord" title="Battle of Hafrsfjord">Battle of Hafrsfjord</a> in 872.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In the Western Isles <a href="/wiki/Ketill_Flatnose" title="Ketill Flatnose">Ketill Flatnose</a> was the dominant figure of the mid 9th century, by which time he had amassed a substantial island realm and made a variety of alliances with other <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Norse</a> leaders. These princelings nominally owed allegiance to the Norwegian crown, although in practice the latter\'s control was fairly limited.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Norse control of the Hebrides was formalised in 1098 when <a href="/wiki/Edgar,_King_of_Scotland" title="Edgar, King of Scotland">Edgar, King of Scotland</a> formally signed the islands over to <a href="/wiki/Magnus_III_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnus III of Norway">Magnus III of Norway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-H102_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H102-89">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The Scottish acceptance of Magnus III as King of the Isles came after the Norwegian king had conquered <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a>, the Hebrides and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> in a swift campaign earlier the same year, directed against the local Norwegian leaders of the various islands petty kingdoms. By capturing the islands Magnus imposed a more direct royal control, although at a price. His <a href="/wiki/Skald" title="Skald">skald</a> Bjorn Cripplehand recorded that in Lewis "fire played high in the heaven" as "flame spouted from the houses" and that in the Uists "the king dyed his sword red in blood".<sup id="cite_ref-H102_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H102-89">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Thompson (1968) provides a more literal translation: "Fire played in the fig-trees of Liodhus; it mounted up to heaven. Far and wide the people were driven to flight. The fire gushed out of the houses".<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson_1968_39_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson_1968_39-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Hebrides were now part of Kingdom of the Isles, whose rulers were themselves vassals of the Kings of Norway. The Kingdom had two parts: the <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">Su\xc3\xb0r-eyjar</i></i> or South Isles  encompassing the <a href="/wiki/Hebrides" title="Hebrides">Hebrides</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>; and the <i><i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">Nor\xc3\xb0r-eyjar</i></i> or North Isles of Orkney and <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a>. This situation lasted until the partitioning of the Western Isles in 1156, at which time the Outer Hebrides remained under Norwegian control while the Inner Hebrides broke out under <a href="/wiki/Somerled" title="Somerled">Somerled</a>, the Norse-Celtic kinsman of the Manx royal house.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the ill-fated 1263 expedition of <a href="/wiki/Haakon_IV_of_Norway" title="Haakon IV of Norway">Haakon IV of Norway</a>, the Outer Hebrides along with the Isle of Man, were yielded to the Kingdom of Scotland a result of the 1266 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Perth" title="Treaty of Perth">Treaty of Perth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Although their contribution to the islands can still be found in personal and placenames, the archaeological record of the Norse period is very limited. The best known find from this time is the <a href="/wiki/Lewis_chessmen" title="Lewis chessmen">Lewis chessmen</a>, which date from the mid 12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scots_rule">Scots rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Scots rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kisimul_Castle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Kisimul_Castle.jpg/220px-Kisimul_Castle.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Kisimul_Castle.jpg/330px-Kisimul_Castle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Kisimul_Castle.jpg/440px-Kisimul_Castle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="427" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kisimul_Castle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kisimul_Castle" title="Kisimul Castle">Kisimul Castle</a>, the ancient seat of <a href="/wiki/Clan_MacNeil" title="Clan MacNeil">Clan MacNeil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Castlebay" title="Castlebay">Castlebay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barra" title="Barra">Barra</a></div></div></div>\n<p>As the Norse era drew to a close the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Norse-speaking</a> princes were gradually replaced by Gaelic-speaking <a href="/wiki/Scottish_clan" title="Scottish clan">clan</a> chiefs including the <a href="/wiki/Clan_MacLeod" title="Clan MacLeod">MacLeods</a> of Lewis and Harris, the <a href="/wiki/Clan_Donald" title="Clan Donald">MacDonalds</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Uist" title="Uist">Uists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clan_MacNeil" title="Clan MacNeil">MacNeil of Barra</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson_1968_39_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson_1968_39-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;Note 4&#93;</a></sup> This transition did little to relieve the islands of internecine strife although by the early 14th century the MacDonald <a href="/wiki/Lord_of_the_Isles" title="Lord of the Isles">Lords of the Isles</a>, based on <a href="/wiki/Islay" title="Islay">Islay</a>, were in theory these chiefs\' feudal superiors and managed to exert some control.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The growing threat that Clan Donald posed to the Scottish crown led to the forcible dissolution of the Lordship of the Isles by <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV</a> in 1493, but although the king had the power to subdue the organised military might of the Hebrides, he and his immediate successors lacked the will or ability to provide an alternative form of governance.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">House of Stuart</a>\'s attempts to control the Outer Hebrides were then at first desultory and little more than punitive expeditions. In 1506 the <a href="/wiki/Marquess_of_Huntly" title="Marquess of Huntly">Earl of Huntly</a> besieged and captured Stornoway Castle using cannon. In 1540 <a href="/wiki/James_V_of_Scotland" title="James V of Scotland">James V</a> himself conducted a royal tour, forcing the clan chiefs to accompany him. There followed a period of peace, but all too soon the clans were at loggerheads again.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1598 King <a href="/wiki/James_VI_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="James VI of Scotland">James VI</a> authorised some "<a href="/wiki/Fife_Adventurers" class="mw-redirect" title="Fife Adventurers">Gentleman Adventurers" from Fife</a> to civilise the "most barbarous Isle of Lewis". Initially successful, the colonists were driven out by local forces commanded by Murdoch and Neil MacLeod, who based their forces on <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Bearasaigh" title="Bearasaigh">Bearasaigh</a></i> in <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Loch R\xc3\xb2g</i>. The colonists tried again in 1605 with the same result but a third attempt in 1607 was more successful, and in due course Stornoway became a <a href="/wiki/Burgh_of_barony" title="Burgh of barony">Burgh of Barony</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> By this time Lewis was held by the Mackenzies of Kintail, (later the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Seaforth" title="Earl of Seaforth">Earls of Seaforth</a>), who pursued a more enlightened approach, investing in fishing in particular. The historian W. C. MacKenzie was moved to write:<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> At the end of the 17th century, the picture we have of Lewis that of a people pursuing their avocation in peace, but not in plenty. The Seaforths&#160;..., besides establishing orderly Government in the island.. had done a great deal to rescue the people from the slough of ignorance and incivility in which they found themselves immersed. But in the sphere of economics their policy apparently was of little service to the community.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The Seaforth\'s royalist inclinations led to Lewis becoming garrisoned during the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a> by <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Cromwell\'s</a> troops, who destroyed the old castle in Stornoway and in 1645 Lewismen fought on the royalist side at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Auldearn" title="Battle of Auldearn">Battle of Auldearn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-R42_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R42-105">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> A new era of Hebridean involvement in the affairs of the wider world was about to commence.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_era">British era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: British era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Old_School_house_Mingulay.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Old_School_house_Mingulay.jpg/220px-Old_School_house_Mingulay.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Old_School_house_Mingulay.jpg/330px-Old_School_house_Mingulay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Old_School_house_Mingulay.jpg/440px-Old_School_house_Mingulay.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="427" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Old_School_house_Mingulay.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Abandoned school house, <a href="/wiki/Mingulay" title="Mingulay">Mingulay</a></div></div></div>\n<p>With the implementation of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a> in 1707 the Hebrides became part of the new <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>, but the clan\'s loyalties to a distant monarch were not strong. A considerable number of islandmen "came out" in support of the Jacobite <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Mar" title="Earl of Mar">Earl of Mar</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_rising_of_1715" title="Jacobite rising of 1715">"15"</a> although the response to the <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_rising_of_1745" title="Jacobite rising of 1745">1745 rising</a> was muted.<sup id="cite_ref-R42_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R42-105">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless the aftermath of the decisive <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Culloden" title="Battle of Culloden">Battle of Culloden</a>, which effectively ended Jacobite hopes of a Stuart restoration, was widely felt. The British government\'s strategy was to estrange the clan chiefs from their kinsmen and turn their descendants into English-speaking landlords whose main concern was the revenues their estates brought rather than the welfare of those who lived on them. This may have brought peace to the islands, but in the following century it came at a terrible price.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Highland_Clearances" title="Highland Clearances">Highland Clearances</a> of the 19th century destroyed communities throughout the <a href="/wiki/Highlands_and_Islands" title="Highlands and Islands">Highlands and Islands</a> as the human populations were evicted and replaced with sheep farms.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> For example, Colonel <a href="/wiki/John_Gordon_(soldier)" title="John Gordon (soldier)">Gordon</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cluny_Castle" title="Cluny Castle">Cluny</a>, owner of Barra, South Uist and Benbecula evicted thousands of islanders using trickery and cruelty and even offered to sell Barra to the government as a penal colony.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Islands such as <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Fuaigh_M%C3%B2r" title="Fuaigh M\xc3\xb2r">Fuaigh M\xc3\xb2r</a></i> were completely cleared of their populations and even today the subject is recalled with bitterness and resentment in some areas.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The position was exacerbated by the failure of the islands\' <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a> industry, which thrived from the 18th century until the end of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> in 1815<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> and large scale emigration became endemic. For example, hundreds left North Uist for <a href="/wiki/Cape_Breton" class="mw-redirect" title="Cape Breton">Cape Breton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The pre-clearance population of the island had been almost 5,000, although by 1841 it had fallen to 3,870 and was only 2,349 by 1931.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USNU_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USNU-112">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For those who remained new economic opportunities emerged through the export of cattle, commercial fishing and tourism. During the summer season in the 1860s and 1870s five thousand inhabitants of Lewis could be found in <a href="/wiki/Wick,_Highland" class="mw-redirect" title="Wick, Highland">Wick</a> on the mainland of Scotland, employed on the fishing boats and at the quaysides.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless emigration and military service became the choice of many<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> and the archipelago\'s populations continued to dwindle throughout the late 19th and 20th centuries. By 2001 the population of North Uist was only 1,271.<sup id="cite_ref-GRO_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GRO-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USNU_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USNU-112">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The work of the <a href="/wiki/Napier_Commission" title="Napier Commission">Napier Commission</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Congested_Districts_Board_(Scotland)" title="Congested Districts Board (Scotland)">Congested Districts Board</a>, and the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Crofters%27_Holdings_(Scotland)_Act_1886" title="Crofters&#39; Holdings (Scotland) Act 1886">Crofting Act of 1886</a> helped, but social unrest continued.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In July 1906 grazing land on <a href="/wiki/Vatersay" title="Vatersay">Vatersay</a> was raided by landless men from Barra and its isles. <a href="/wiki/Emily_Gordon_Cathcart" title="Emily Gordon Cathcart">Lady Gordon Cathcart</a> took legal action against the "raiders" but the visiting judge took the view that she had neglected her duties as a landowner and that "long indifference to the necessities of the cottars had gone far to drive them to exasperation".<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Millennia of continuous occupation notwithstanding, many of the remoter islands were abandoned \xe2\x80\x94 <a href="/wiki/Mingulay" title="Mingulay">Mingulay</a> in 1912, <a href="/wiki/Hirta" title="Hirta">Hirta</a> in 1930, and <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Ceann_Iar" title="Ceann Iar">Ceann Iar</a></i> in 1942 among them. This process involved a transition from these places being perceived as relatively self-sufficient agricultural economies<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> to a view becoming held by both island residents and outsiders alike that they lacked the essential services of a modern industrial economy.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There were gradual economic improvements, among the most visible of which was the replacement of the traditional thatched <a href="/wiki/Blackhouse" title="Blackhouse">blackhouse</a> with accommodation of a more modern design. The creation of the <a href="/wiki/Highlands_and_Islands_Development_Board" class="mw-redirect" title="Highlands and Islands Development Board">Highlands and Islands Development Board</a> and the discovery of substantial deposits of <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_oil" title="North Sea oil">North Sea oil</a> in 1965, the establishment of a unitary local authority for the islands in 1975 and more recently the <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_Scotland" title="Renewable energy in Scotland">renewables sector</a> have all contributed to a degree of economic stability in recent decades. The <a href="/wiki/Arnish_yard" class="mw-redirect" title="Arnish yard">Arnish yard</a> has had a chequered history but has been a significant employer in both the oil and renewables industries.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Comhairle nan Eilean Siar</i>, the local authority, employs 2,000 people, making it the largest employer in the Outer Hebrides.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> See also the "<i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Innse Gall</i> area plan 2010"<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> and the <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Comhairle</i>\'s "Factfile \xe2\x80\x93 Economy".<sup id="cite_ref-FF_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FF-122">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stornoway_Harbour.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Stornoway_Harbour.jpg/220px-Stornoway_Harbour.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Stornoway_Harbour.jpg/330px-Stornoway_Harbour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Stornoway_Harbour.jpg/440px-Stornoway_Harbour.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stornoway_Harbour.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Stornoway" title="Stornoway">Stornoway</a> Harbour</div></div></div>\n<p>Modern commercial activities centre on tourism, <a href="/wiki/Crofting" title="Crofting">crofting</a>, fishing, and weaving including the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Harris_tweed" class="mw-redirect" title="Harris tweed">Harris tweed</a>. Some of the larger islands have <a href="/wiki/Development_trust" title="Development trust">development trusts</a> that support the local economy and, in striking contrast to the 19th and 20th century domination by absentee landlords, more than two thirds of the Western Isles population now lives on community-owned estates.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> However the economic position of the islands remains relatively precarious. The Western Isles, including Stornoway, are defined by <a href="/wiki/Highlands_and_Islands_Enterprise" title="Highlands and Islands Enterprise">Highlands and Islands Enterprise</a> as an economically "Fragile Area" and they have an estimated trade deficit of some \xc2\xa3163.4 million. Overall, the area is relatively reliant on primary industries and the public sector, and fishing and fish farming in particular are vulnerable to environmental impacts, changing market pressures, and European legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-FF_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FF-122">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is some optimism about the possibility of future developments in, for example, renewable energy generation, tourism, and education, and after declines in the 20th century the population has stabilised since 2003, although it is ageing.<sup id="cite_ref-FF_122-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FF-122">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics_and_local_government">Politics and local government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Politics and local government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Comhairle_nan_Eilean_Siar" title="Comhairle nan Eilean Siar">Comhairle nan Eilean Siar</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Edward_Scott_Junior_Secondary_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1390447.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Sir_Edward_Scott_Junior_Secondary_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1390447.jpg/220px-Sir_Edward_Scott_Junior_Secondary_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1390447.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Sir_Edward_Scott_Junior_Secondary_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1390447.jpg/330px-Sir_Edward_Scott_Junior_Secondary_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1390447.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Sir_Edward_Scott_Junior_Secondary_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1390447.jpg/440px-Sir_Edward_Scott_Junior_Secondary_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1390447.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Edward_Scott_Junior_Secondary_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1390447.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sir Edward Scott Secondary School, <a href="/wiki/Tarbert,_Harris" title="Tarbert, Harris">Tarbert, Isle of Harris</a></div></div></div>\n<p>From the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_(Scotland)_Act_1889" title="Local Government (Scotland) Act 1889">Local Government (Scotland) Act 1889</a> to 1975 Lewis formed part of the <a href="/wiki/Counties_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Counties of Scotland">county</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ross_and_Cromarty" title="Ross and Cromarty">Ross and Cromarty</a> and the rest of the archipelago, including <a href="/wiki/Harris,_Outer_Hebrides" class="mw-redirect" title="Harris, Outer Hebrides">Harris</a>, was part of <a href="/wiki/Inverness-shire" title="Inverness-shire">Inverness-shire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Outer Hebrides became a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_authority" title="Unitary authority">unitary council</a> area in 1975, although in most of the rest of Scotland similar unitary councils were not established until 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Since then, the islands have formed one of the <a href="/wiki/Subdivisions_of_Scotland" title="Subdivisions of Scotland">32 unitary council areas</a> that now cover the whole country, with the council officially known by its Gaelic name, <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Comhairle_nan_Eilean_Siar" title="Comhairle nan Eilean Siar">Comhairle nan Eilean Siar</a></i></i> under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_(Gaelic_Names)_(Scotland)_Act_1997" title="Local Government (Gaelic Names) (Scotland) Act 1997">Local Government (Gaelic Names) (Scotland) Act 1997</a>. The council has its base in Stornoway on Lewis and is often known locally simply as "the <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Comhairle</i>" or <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">a\' Chomhairle</i></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Comhairle</i> is one of only three Councils in Scotland with a majority of elected members who are <a href="/wiki/Independent_politician" title="Independent politician">independents</a>. The other independent-run councils are <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a>. <a href="/wiki/Moray" title="Moray">Moray</a> is run by a Conservative/Independent coalition.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The name for the British Parliament constituency covering this area is <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Na_h-Eileanan_an_Iar_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Na h-Eileanan an Iar (UK Parliament constituency)">Na h-Eileanan an Iar</a></i>, the seat being held by <a href="/wiki/Angus_MacNeil" title="Angus MacNeil">Angus MacNeil</a> MP since 2005, while the Scottish Parliament constituency for the area is <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Na_h-Eileanan_an_Iar_(Scottish_Parliament_constituency)" title="Na h-Eileanan an Iar (Scottish Parliament constituency)">Na h-Eileanan an Iar</a></i>, the incumbent being <a href="/wiki/Alasdair_Allan" title="Alasdair Allan">Alasdair Allan</a> MSP.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence_referendum" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish independence referendum">Scottish independence referendum</a> has led some islanders to call for a debate on the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_position_of_the_Western_Isles" class="mw-redirect" title="Constitutional position of the Western Isles">constitutional position of the Western Isles</a>, linked with similar initiatives in <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scottish_Gaelic_language">Scottish Gaelic language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Scottish Gaelic language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Outer Hebrides have historically been a very strong <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> (<i>G\xc3\xa0idhlig</i>) speaking area. Both the 1901 and 1921 census reported that all parishes were over 75% Gaelic speaking, including areas of high population density such as Stornoway. However, the <a href="/wiki/Education_(Scotland)_Act_1872" title="Education (Scotland) Act 1872">Education (Scotland) Act 1872</a> mandated English-only education, and is now recognised as having dealt a major blow to the language. People still living can recall being beaten for speaking Gaelic in school.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, by 1971 most areas were still more than 75% Gaelic speaking \xe2\x80\x93 with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Stornoway" title="Stornoway">Stornoway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benbecula" title="Benbecula">Benbecula</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Uist" title="South Uist">South Uist</a> at 50-74%.<sup id="cite_ref-Macpp_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macpp-133">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 2001 census, each island overall was over 50% Gaelic speaking \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/South_Uist" title="South Uist">South Uist</a> (71%), <a href="/wiki/Harris,_Outer_Hebrides" class="mw-redirect" title="Harris, Outer Hebrides">Harris</a> (69%), <a href="/wiki/Barra" title="Barra">Barra</a> (68%), <a href="/wiki/North_Uist" title="North Uist">North Uist</a> (67%), <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Lewis" title="Isle of Lewis">Lewis</a> (56%) and <a href="/wiki/Benbecula" title="Benbecula">Benbecula</a> (56%). With 59.3% of Gaelic speakers or a total of 15,723 speakers, this made the Outer Hebrides the most strongly coherent Gaelic speaking area in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Macpp_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macpp-133">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most areas were between 60-74% Gaelic speaking and the areas with the highest density of over 80% are <a href="/wiki/Scalpay,_Outer_Hebrides" title="Scalpay, Outer Hebrides">Scalpay near Harris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newtonferry" title="Newtonferry">Newtonferry</a> and Kildonan, whilst <a href="/wiki/Daliburgh" title="Daliburgh">Daliburgh</a>, Linshader, <a href="/wiki/Eriskay" title="Eriskay">Eriskay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brue" title="Brue">Brue</a>, Boisdale, <a href="/wiki/Harris,_Outer_Hebrides" class="mw-redirect" title="Harris, Outer Hebrides">West Harris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ardveenish" title="Ardveenish">Ardveenish</a>, Soval, <a href="/wiki/Ness,_Western_Isles" class="mw-redirect" title="Ness, Western Isles">Ness</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bragar" title="Bragar">Bragar</a> all have more than 75%. The areas with the lowest density of speakers are Stornoway (44%), Braigh (41%), <a href="/wiki/Melbost" title="Melbost">Melbost</a> (41%), and <a href="/wiki/Balivanich" title="Balivanich">Balivanich</a> (37%).<sup id="cite_ref-Macpp_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macpp-133">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Language_(Scotland)_Act_2005" title="Gaelic Language (Scotland) Act 2005">Gaelic Language (Scotland) Act</a> was enacted by the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a> in 2005 to provide continuing support for the language.<sup id="cite_ref-about_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-about-135">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> However, by 2011 the overall percentage of Gaelic speakers in the Outer Hebrides had fallen to 52%.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg/220px-Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg/330px-Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg/440px-Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leaving_Lochmaddy_-_geograph.org.uk_-_929584.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Caledonian_MacBrayne" title="Caledonian MacBrayne">Caledonian MacBrayne</a> ferry  <a href="/wiki/MV_Hebrides" title="MV Hebrides">MV <i>Hebrides</i></a> leaving <a href="/wiki/Lochmaddy" title="Lochmaddy">Lochmaddy</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Uist" title="North Uist">North Uist</a> en route for <a href="/wiki/Skye" title="Skye">Skye</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Scheduled <a href="/wiki/Ferry" title="Ferry">ferry</a> services between the Outer Hebrides and the Scottish Mainland and Inner Hebrides operate on the following routes:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Oban" title="Oban">Oban</a> to <a href="/wiki/Castlebay" title="Castlebay">Castlebay</a> on <a href="/wiki/Barra" title="Barra">Barra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lochboisdale" title="Lochboisdale">Lochboisdale</a> on <a href="/wiki/South_Uist" title="South Uist">South Uist</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Uig,_Skye" title="Uig, Skye">Uig</a> on <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Skye" class="mw-redirect" title="Isle of Skye">Skye</a> to <a href="/wiki/Tarbert,_Harris" title="Tarbert, Harris">Tarbert</a> on <a href="/wiki/Harris,_Outer_Hebrides" class="mw-redirect" title="Harris, Outer Hebrides">Harris</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Uig,_Skye" title="Uig, Skye">Uig</a> on <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Skye" class="mw-redirect" title="Isle of Skye">Skye</a> to <a href="/wiki/Lochmaddy" title="Lochmaddy">Lochmaddy</a> on <a href="/wiki/North_Uist" title="North Uist">North Uist</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ullapool" title="Ullapool">Ullapool</a> to <a href="/wiki/Stornoway,_Outer_Hebrides" class="mw-redirect" title="Stornoway, Outer Hebrides">Stornoway</a> on <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Lewis" title="Isle of Lewis">Lewis</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tiree" title="Tiree">Tiree</a> to <a href="/wiki/Castlebay" title="Castlebay">Castlebay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barra" title="Barra">Barra</a> (summer only).</li></ul>\n<p>Other ferries operate between some of the islands.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>National Rail services are available for onward journeys, from stations at <a href="/wiki/Oban" title="Oban">Oban</a>, which has direct services to Glasgow. However, parliamentary approval notwithstanding, plans in the 1890s to lay <a href="/wiki/Garve_and_Ullapool_Railway" title="Garve and Ullapool Railway">a railway connection to Ullapool</a> were unable to obtain sufficient funding.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are scheduled flights from <a href="/wiki/Stornoway_Airport" title="Stornoway Airport">Stornoway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benbecula_Airport" title="Benbecula Airport">Benbecula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barra_Airport_(Scotland)" title="Barra Airport (Scotland)">Barra</a> airports both inter-island and to the mainland. Barra\'s airport is claimed to be the only one in the world to have scheduled flights landing on a beach. At high water the runways are under the sea so flight times vary with the tide.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bus_na_Comhairle">Bus na Comhairle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Bus na Comhairle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><b>Bus na Comhairle</b> (meaning "Bus of the Council") is the council-owned local bus company of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Isles" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Isles">Western Isles</a> of Scotland. The company serves <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Harris" title="Lewis and Harris">Lewis and Harris</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Uist" title="North Uist">North Uist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benbecula" title="Benbecula">Benbecula</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Uist" title="South Uist">South Uist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eriskay" title="Eriskay">Eriskay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barra" title="Barra">Barra</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipwrecks">Shipwrecks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Shipwrecks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Admiralty-yacht-HMS-Iolaire-ship-Amalthaea-1908.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Admiralty-yacht-HMS-Iolaire-ship-Amalthaea-1908.jpg/220px-Admiralty-yacht-HMS-Iolaire-ship-Amalthaea-1908.jpg" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Admiralty-yacht-HMS-Iolaire-ship-Amalthaea-1908.jpg/330px-Admiralty-yacht-HMS-Iolaire-ship-Amalthaea-1908.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Admiralty-yacht-HMS-Iolaire-ship-Amalthaea-1908.jpg/440px-Admiralty-yacht-HMS-Iolaire-ship-Amalthaea-1908.jpg 2x" data-file-width="648" data-file-height="374" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Admiralty-yacht-HMS-Iolaire-ship-Amalthaea-1908.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">Admiralty</a> yacht <a href="/wiki/HMY_Iolaire" title="HMY Iolaire">HMS <i>Iolaire</i></a></div></div></div>\n<p>The archipelago is exposed to wind and tide, and <a href="/wiki/Lighthouse" title="Lighthouse">lighthouses</a> are sited as an aid to navigation at locations from Barra Head in the south to the Butt of Lewis in the north.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> There are numerous sites of wrecked ships, and the <a href="/wiki/Flannan_Isles" title="Flannan Isles">Flannan Isles</a> are the location of an enduring mystery that occurred in December 1900, when all three lighthouse keepers vanished without trace.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Annie Jane</i>, a three-masted immigrant ship out of <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> bound for <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal, Canada</a>, struck rocks off the West Beach of Vatersay during a storm on Tuesday 28 September 1853. Within ten minutes the ship began to founder and break up casting 450 people into the raging sea. In spite of the conditions, islanders tried to rescue the passengers and crew. The remains of 350 men, women and children were buried in the dunes behind the beach and a small cairn and monument marks the site.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The tiny Beasts of Holm off the east coast of Lewis were the site of the sinking of <a href="/wiki/HMS_Iolaire" class="mw-redirect" title="HMS Iolaire">HMS&#160;<i>Iolaire </i></a> during the first few hours of 1919,<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> one of the worst maritime disasters in United Kingdom waters during the 20th century. <a href="/wiki/Calvay" title="Calvay">Calvay</a> in the Sound of Barra provided the inspiration for <a href="/wiki/Compton_Mackenzie" title="Compton Mackenzie">Compton Mackenzie</a>\'s  novel <i><a href="/wiki/Whisky_Galore_(novel)" title="Whisky Galore (novel)">Whisky Galore</a></i> after the <a href="/wiki/SS_Politician" title="SS Politician">SS&#160;<i>Politician</i></a> ran aground there with a cargo of <a href="/wiki/Single_malt_whisky" title="Single malt whisky">single malt</a> in 1941.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Religion.2C_culture_and_sport"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion,_culture_and_sport">Religion, culture and sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Religion, culture and sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_Outer_Hebrides" title="Religion in the Outer Hebrides">Religion in the Outer Hebrides</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> has deep roots in the Western Isles, but owing mainly to the different allegiances of the clans in the past, the people in the northern islands (Lewis, Harris, North Uist) have historically been predominantly <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterian</a>, and those of the southern islands (Benbecula, South Uist, Barra) predominantly <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the time of the 2001 Census, 42% of the population identified themselves as being affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>, with 13% Roman Catholic and 28% with other Christian churches. Many of this last group belong to the <a href="/wiki/Free_Church_of_Scotland_(post_1900)" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Church of Scotland (post 1900)">Free Church of Scotland</a>, known for its strict observance of the <a href="/wiki/Sabbath_in_Christianity" title="Sabbath in Christianity">Sabbath</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> 11% stated that they had no religion.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;Note 5&#93;</a></sup> This made the Western Isles the Scottish council area with the smallest percentage of non-religious in the population. There are also small <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Episcopal_Church" title="Scottish Episcopal Church">Episcopalian</a> congregations in Lewis and Harris and the Outer Hebrides are part of the <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Argyll_and_The_Isles_(Episcopal)" title="Diocese of Argyll and The Isles (Episcopal)">Diocese of Argyll and The Isles</a> in both the Episcopalian and Catholic traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gaelic_music" title="Gaelic music">Gaelic music</a> is popular in the islands and the Lewis and Harris Traditional Music Society plays an active role in promoting the genre.<sup id="cite_ref-RC39_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RC39-151">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A8is_Bharraigh" title="F\xc3\xa8is Bharraigh">F\xc3\xa8is Bharraigh</a> began in 1981 with the aim of  developing the practice and study of the Gaelic language, literature, music, drama and culture on the islands of Barra and Vatersay. A two-week festival,  it has inspired 43 other <i>feisean</i> throughout Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> The Lewis <a href="/wiki/Great_Highland_Bagpipe" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Highland Bagpipe">Pipe Band</a> was founded in 1904 and the Lewis and Harris Piping Society in 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-RC39_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RC39-151">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Outdoor activities including rugby, football, golf, shinty, fishing, riding, canoeing, athletics, and multi-sports are popular in the Western Isles. The Hebridean Challenge is an adventure race run in five daily stages, which takes place along the length of the islands and includes hill and road running, road and mountain biking, short sea swims and demanding sea kayaking sections. There are four main sports centres: <i>Ionad Spors Leodhais</i> in Stornoway, which has a 25 m swimming pool; Harris Sports Centre; <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lionacleit&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lionacleit (page does not exist)">Lionacleit</a> Sports Centre on Benbecula; and <a href="/wiki/Castlebay" title="Castlebay">Castlebay</a> Sports Centre on Barra. The Western Isles is a member of the <a href="/wiki/International_Island_Games_Association" title="International Island Games Association">International Island Games Association</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>South Uist is home to the Askernish Golf Course. The oldest links in the Outer Hebrides, it was designed by <a href="/wiki/Old_Tom_Morris" title="Old Tom Morris">Old Tom Morris</a>. Although it was in use until the 1930s, its existence was largely forgotten until 2005 and it is now being restored to Morris\'s original design.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/I_Know_Where_I%27m_Going!" class="mw-redirect" title="I Know Where I&#39;m Going!">I Know Where I\'m Going!</a></i> is a 1945 British drama/romance film set mostly in the Outer Hebrides, depicting local lifestyles and speech.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hebridean_Myths_and_Legends" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebridean Myths and Legends">Hebridean Myths and Legends</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_places_in_the_Western_Isles" title="List of places in the Western Isles">List of places in the Western Isles</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_the_Kingdom_of_the_Isles" title="List of rulers of the Kingdom of the Isles">List of rulers of the Kingdom of the Isles</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_Scotland" title="List of islands of Scotland">List of islands of Scotland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lj%C3%B3t%C3%B3lfr" title="Lj\xc3\xb3t\xc3\xb3lfr">Lj\xc3\xb3t\xc3\xb3lfr</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Leod" title="Leod">Leod</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_Category_A_listed_buildings_in_the_Western_Isles" title="List of Category A listed buildings in the Western Isles">List of Category A listed buildings in the Western Isles</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Comhairle_nan_Eilean_Siar_election,_2012" title="Comhairle nan Eilean Siar election, 2012">Comhairle nan Eilean Siar election, 2012</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Virtual_Hebrides" title="Virtual Hebrides">Virtual Hebrides</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Constitutional_status_of_Orkney,_Shetland_and_the_Western_Isles" title="Constitutional status of Orkney, Shetland and the Western Isles">Constitutional status of Orkney, Shetland and the Western Isles</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_1_May_1185" title="Solar eclipse of 1 May 1185">Solar eclipse of 1 May 1185</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Murray (1973) notes that "Western Isles" has tended to mean "Outer Hebrides" since the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Na_h-Eileanan_an_Iar_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Na h-Eileanan an Iar (UK Parliament constituency)">Na h-Eileanan an Iar</a> or Western Isles parliamentary constituency in 1918. The phrase can also be used to refer to the <a href="/wiki/Hebrides" title="Hebrides">Hebrides</a> in general. Murray also notes that "Gneiss Islands"\xe2\x80\x94a reference to the underlying geology \xe2\x80\x93 is another name used to refer to the Outer Hebrides but that its use is "confined to books".<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This tidal isle is at (<a href="/wiki/Ordnance_Survey_National_Grid" title="Ordnance Survey National Grid">grid reference</a> <span style="white-space: nowrap" class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="https://tools.wmflabs.org/os/coor_g/?pagename=Outer_Hebrides&amp;params=NF860580%29_region%3AGB_scale%3A25000">NF860580)</a></span> and the evidence of both Ordnance Survey maps and photographs (e.g. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.geograph.org.uk/photo/445166">"Houses on <i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Seana Bhaile</i>"</a> Geograph. Retrieved 10 August 2009) indicates a resident population. There is even a name, <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Seana Bhaile</i></i> (English: "Old Town") for the main settlement. However, neither the census nor the main reference work (Haswell-Smith 2004) refer to the island. Its population is presumably included in nearby Grimsay by the census.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lewisian gneiss is sometimes described as the oldest rock found in Europe, but trondhjemite gneiss recently measured at Siurua in Finland has been dated to 3.4\xe2\x80\x933.5 Ga.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-98"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-98">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The transitional relationships between Norse and Gaelic-speaking rulers are complex. The <i><i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text"><a href="/wiki/Norse%E2%80%93Gaels" title="Norse\xe2\x80\x93Gaels">Gall-Gh\xc3\xa0idhels</a></i></i> who dominated much of the Irish Sea region and western Scotland at this time were of joint Gaelic and Scandinavian origin. When Somerled wrested the southern Inner Hebrides from <a href="/wiki/Godred_II_Olafsson" class="mw-redirect" title="Godred II Olafsson">Godred the Black</a> in 1156, this was the beginnings of a break with nominal Norse rule in the Hebrides. Godred remained the ruler of Mann and the Outer Hebrides, but two years later Somerled\'s invasion of the former caused him to flee to Norway. Norse control was further weakened in the ensuring century, but the Hebrides were not formally ceded by Norway until 1266.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The transitions from one language to another are also complex. For example, many Scandinavian sources from this period of time typically refer to individuals as having  a Scandinavian first name and a Gaelic by-name.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-148"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-148">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> The 2001 census statistics used are based on local authority areas but do not specifically identify Free Church or Episcopal adherents. 4% of the respondents did not answer this census question and the total for all other religions combined is 1 per cent.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Hebrides&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://ons.maps.arcgis.com/home/item.html?id=a79de233ad254a6d9f76298e666abb2b">"Standard Area Measurements (2016) for Administrative Areas in the United Kingdom"</a>. <a href="/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics" title="Office for National Statistics">Office for National Statistics</a>. 1 February 2017<span class="reference-accessdate">.
